Chiang flew into the area to lead his armies against Mao, but the Communists outmanoeuvred him and crossed the <a href="/wiki/Jinsha_River" title="Jinsha River">Jinsha River</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> Faced with the more difficult task of crossing the <a href="/wiki/Tatu_River" title="Tatu River" class="mw-redirect">Tatu River</a>, they managed it by fighting a battle over the <a href="/wiki/Luding_Bridge" title="Luding Bridge">Luding Bridge</a> in May, taking <a href="/wiki/Luding_County" title="Luding County">Luding</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup> Marching through the mountain ranges around <a href="/wiki/Ma%27anshan" title="Ma'anshan">Ma'anshan</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> in Moukung, Western Szechuan they encountered the 50,000-strong CPC Fourth Front Army of <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Guotao" title="Zhang Guotao">Zhang Guotao</a>, together proceeding to Maoerhkai and then <a href="/wiki/Gansu" title="Gansu">Gansu</a>. However, Zhang and Mao disagreed over what to do; the latter wished to proceed to Shaanxi, while Zhang wanted to flee east to <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sikkim" title="Sikkim">Sikkim</a>, far from the KMT threat. It was agreed that they would go their separate ways, with <a href="/wiki/Zhu_De" title="Zhu De">Zhu De</a> joining Zhang.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup> Mao's forces proceeded north, through hundreds of miles of <a href="/wiki/Mongolian-Manchurian_grassland" title="Mongolian-Manchurian grassland">Grasslands</a>, an area of quagmire where they were attacked by <a href="/wiki/Manchu_people" title="Manchu people">Manchu tribesman</a> and where many soldiers succumbed to famine and disease.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Feigon_2002_61_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feigon_2002_61-138"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> Finally reaching <a href="/wiki/Shaanxi" title="Shaanxi">Shaanxi</a>, they fought off both the KMT and an Islamic cavalry militia before crossing over the <a href="/wiki/Min_Mountains" title="Min Mountains">Min Mountains</a> and Mount Liupan and reaching the Shenshi Soviet; only 7-8000 had survived.<sup id="cite_ref-Feigon_2002_61_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feigon_2002_61-138"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup> The Long March cemented Mao's status as the dominant figure in the party. In November 1935, he was named chairman of the Military Commission. From this point onward, Mao was the Communist Party's undisputed leader, even though he would not become party chairman until 1943.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Many if not most of the events as later described by Mao and which the CPC claims are true are seen as false by historians such as Jung Chang. During the decade spent researching the book, <i>Mao: The Unknown Story</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-141"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> for instance, Chang found evidence that there was no battle at Luding and that the CPC crossed the bridge unopposed. Chang interviewed an eye witness to the crossing of the Dadu (Tatu) River at Luding, Mrs Zhu De, then 93 years old, who recalled no deaths, except for two people who fell from the bridge at Luding while repairing it. Chang also points out the contradictions in the version of events as told by the CPC, which said the bridge was taken by a suicide attack by 22 men, but that these men were also present at a ceremony following the crossing of the bridge.<sup id="cite_ref-ChangHalliday_a_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChangHalliday_a-142"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Chang and Halliday also dispute the Communist Party of China's official version by claiming that far from the Long March being a masterful piece of strategy by the CPC, it was in fact devised by Chiang Kai-shek, leader of the KMT. Chiang's aim was to give the CPC an easy route to follow through warlord controlled areas. Hemmed in by Nationalist troops on three sides, the CPC was forced to follow the route dictated by the KMT. The aim of this was to allow KMT forces to follow the reds into warlord controlled areas such as Sichuan and win over warlords scared of the sudden arrival of the Communist force. The only glitch in this plan came when Mao refused to follow the easy route into Sichuan where he was to meet up with a red army much larger than his own and led by a more senior CPC member, Chang Kuo Tao. Mao recognised the threat Chang posed to his rising position in the CPC and doubled back to give himself time to further cement his political power, causing the needless deaths of thousands of his own troops.<sup id="cite_ref-ChangHalliday_a_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChangHalliday_a-142"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Chang and Halliday also claim that Mao and other top CPC leaders did not walk the Long March, but were carried on litters&#160;– Mao himself told his staff that being carried on the Long March gave him much time to read – with the litter bearers' knees being worn to the bone when forced to carry Mao up mountains.<sup id="cite_ref-ChangHalliday_a_142-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChangHalliday_a-142"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alliance_with_the_Kuomintang:_1935.E2.80.931940">Alliance with the Kuomintang: 1935–1940</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Second_Sino-Japanese_War" title="Second Sino-Japanese War">Second Sino-Japanese War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1946_Mao_and_Chiang.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/1946_Mao_and_Chiang.jpg/220px-1946_Mao_and_Chiang.jpg" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/1946_Mao_and_Chiang.jpg/330px-1946_Mao_and_Chiang.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/1946_Mao_and_Chiang.jpg/440px-1946_Mao_and_Chiang.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="336" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1946_Mao_and_Chiang.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In an effort to defeat the Japanese, Mao (left) agreed to collaborate with Chiang (right).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Arriving at the <a href="/wiki/Yan%27an" title="Yan'an">Yan'an</a> Soviet during October 1935, Mao's troops settled in Pao An. Remaining there till spring 1936, they developed links with local communities, redistributed and farmed the land, offered medical treatment and began literacy programs.<sup id="cite_ref-Feigon_2002_61_138-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feigon_2002_61-138"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schram_p193_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schram_p193-143"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup> Mao now commanded 15,000 soldiers, boosted by the arrival of <a href="/wiki/He_Long" title="He Long">He Long</a>'s men from Hunan and the armies of Zhu Den and Zhang Guotao, returning from Tibet.<sup id="cite_ref-Schram_p193_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schram_p193-143"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup> In February 1936 they established the North West Anti-Japanese Red Army University in Yan'an, through which they trained increasing numbers of new recruits.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup> In January 1937 they began the "anti-Japanese expedition", sending groups of guerrilla fighters into Japanese-controlled territory to undertake sporadic attacks,<sup id="cite_ref-Schram1966_p20_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schram1966_p20-146"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup> while in May 1937, a Communist Conference was held in Yan'an to discuss the situation.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup> Western reporters also arrived in the "Border Region" (as the Soviet had been renamed); most notable were <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Snow" title="Edgar Snow">Edgar Snow</a>, who used his experiences as a basis for <i><a href="/wiki/Red_Star_Over_China" title="Red Star Over China">Red Star Over China</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Agnes_Smedley" title="Agnes Smedley">Agnes Smedley</a>, whose accounts brought international attention to Mao's cause.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On the Long March, Mao's wife He Zizen had been injured from a shrapnel wound to the head, and so traveled to Moscow for medical treatment; Mao proceeded to divorce her and marry an actress, <a href="/wiki/Jiang_Qing" title="Jiang Qing">Jiang Qing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Schram_p208_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schram_p208-118"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup> Mao moved into a cave-house and spent much of his time reading, tending his garden and theorizing.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup> He came to believe that the Red Army alone was unable to defeat the Japanese, and that a Communist-led "government of national defence" should be formed with the KMT and other "bourgeois nationalist" elements to achieve this goal.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup> Although despising Chiang Kai-shek as a "traitor to the nation",<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup> on May 5 he telegrammed the Military Council of the Nanking National Government proposing a military alliance, a course of action advocated by Stalin.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> Although Chiang intended to ignore Mao's message and continue the civil war, he was arrested by one of his own generals, <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Xueliang" title="Zhang Xueliang">Zhang Xueliang</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi'an">Xi'an</a>, leading to the <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an_Incident" title="Xi'an Incident">Xi'an Incident</a>; Zhang forced Chiang to discuss the issue with the Communists, resulting in the formation of a <a href="/wiki/Second_United_Front_(China)" title="Second United Front (China)" class="mw-redirect">United Front</a> with concessions on both sides on December 25, 1937.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mao1938a.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Mao1938a.jpg/220px-Mao1938a.jpg" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Mao1938a.jpg/330px-Mao1938a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Mao1938a.jpg/440px-Mao1938a.jpg 2x" data-file-width="483" data-file-height="367" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mao1938a.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mao in 1938, writing <i>On Protracted War</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Japanese had taken both <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanking (Nanjing)</a>—resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Nanking_Massacre" title="Nanking Massacre">Nanking Massacre</a>, an atrocity Mao never spoke of all his life—pushing the Kuomintang government inland to <a href="/wiki/Chongqing" title="Chongqing">Chungking</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup> The Japanese's brutality led increasing numbers of Chinese joining the fight, with the Red Army growing from 50,000 to 500,000.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Carter1976_p105_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carter1976_p105-158"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup> In August 1938, the Red Army formed the <a href="/wiki/New_Fourth_Army" title="New Fourth Army">New Fourth Army</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Eighth_Route_Army" title="Eighth Route Army">Eighth Route Army</a>, which were nominally under the command of Chiang's <a href="/wiki/National_Revolutionary_Army" title="National Revolutionary Army">National Revolutionary Army</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup> In August 1940, the Red Army initiated the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Regiments_Campaign" title="Hundred Regiments Campaign" class="mw-redirect">Hundred Regiments Campaign</a>, in which 400,000 troops attacked the Japanese simultaneously in five provinces; a military success, it resulted in the death of 20,000 Japanese, the disruption of railways and the loss of a coal mine.<sup id="cite_ref-Carter1976_p105_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carter1976_p105-158"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a></sup> From his base in Yan'an, Mao authored several texts for his troops, including <i>Philosophy of Revolution</i>, which offered an introduction to the Marxist theory of knowledge, <i>Protracted Warfare</i>, which dealt with guerilla and mobile military tactics, and <i>New Democracy</i>, which laid forward ideas for China's future.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Resuming_civil_war:_1940.E2.80.931949">Resuming civil war: 1940–1949</span></h3>
<p>In 1944, the Americans sent a special diplomatic envoy, called the <a href="/wiki/Dixie_Mission" title="Dixie Mission">Dixie Mission</a>, to the Communist Party of China. According to Edwin Moise, in <i>Modern China: A History 2nd Edition</i>:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Most of the Americans were favourably impressed. The CPC seemed less corrupt, more unified, and more vigorous in its resistance to Japan than the KMT. United States fliers shot down over North China&#160;... confirmed to their superiors that the CPC was both strong and popular over a broad area. In the end, the contacts with the USA developed with the CPC led to very little.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>After the end of World War II, the U.S. continued their military assistance to Chiang Kai-shek and his KMT government forces against the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Liberation_Army" title="People's Liberation Army">People's Liberation Army</a> (PLA) led by Mao Zedong in the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Civil_War" title="Chinese Civil War">civil war</a> for control of China. Likewise, the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> gave quasi-covert support to Mao by their occupation of north east China, which allowed the PLA to move in en masse and took large supplies of arms left by the Japanese's <a href="/wiki/Kwantung_Army" title="Kwantung Army">Kwantung Army</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Mao, to enhance the Red Army's military operations named his close associate, then General <a href="/wiki/Zhu_De" title="Zhu De">Zhu De</a> to be its Commander-in-Chief under his supervision and control as the Chairman of the Communist Party of China that has jurisdiction over the said army through the party's Central Military Commission headed by future Premier <a href="/wiki/Zhou_En-Lai" title="Zhou En-Lai" class="mw-redirect">Zhou En-Lai</a></p>
<p>In 1948, under direct orders from Mao, the People's Liberation Army starved out the Kuomintang forces occupying the city of <a href="/wiki/Changchun" title="Changchun">Changchun</a>. At least 160,000 civilians are believed to have perished during <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Changchun" title="Siege of Changchun">the siege</a>, which lasted from June until October. PLA lieutenant colonel Zhang Zhenglu, who documented the siege in his book <i><a href="/wiki/White_Snow,_Red_Blood" title="White Snow, Red Blood">White Snow, Red Blood</a></i>, compared it to <a href="/wiki/Hiroshima" title="Hiroshima">Hiroshima</a>: "The casualties were about the same. Hiroshima took nine seconds; Changchun took five months."<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup> On January 21, 1949, Kuomintang forces suffered great losses in decisive battles against Mao's forces.<sup id="cite_ref-Palestini2011_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palestini2011-163"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a></sup> In the early morning of December 10, 1949, PLA troops laid siege to <a href="/wiki/Chongqing" title="Chongqing">Chongqing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chengdu" title="Chengdu">Chengdu</a> on <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">mainland China</a>, and Chiang Kai-shek fled from the mainland to <a href="/wiki/Formosa" title="Formosa" class="mw-redirect">Formosa</a> (Taiwan).<sup id="cite_ref-Palestini2011_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palestini2011-163"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Perkins2013_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Perkins2013-164"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Leadership_of_China">Leadership of China</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mao_Proclaiming_New_China.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Mao_Proclaiming_New_China.JPG/220px-Mao_Proclaiming_New_China.JPG" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Mao_Proclaiming_New_China.JPG/330px-Mao_Proclaiming_New_China.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Mao_Proclaiming_New_China.JPG/440px-Mao_Proclaiming_New_China.JPG 2x" data-file-width="771" data-file-height="566" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mao_Proclaiming_New_China.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mao Zedong declares the founding of the modern People's Republic of China, October 1, 1949.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The People's Republic of China was established on October 1, 1949. It was the culmination of over two decades of civil and international wars. Mao famously announced: "We (the Chinese people) have stood up."<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Mao took up residence in <a href="/wiki/Zhongnanhai" title="Zhongnanhai">Zhongnanhai</a>, a compound next to the <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_City" title="Forbidden City">Forbidden City</a> in Beijing, and there he ordered the construction of an indoor swimming pool and other buildings. Mao's physician <a href="/wiki/Li_Zhisui" title="Li Zhisui">Li Zhisui</a> described him as conducting business either in bed or by the side of the pool, preferring not to wear formal clothes unless absolutely necessary.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a></sup> Li's book, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Private_Life_of_Chairman_Mao" title="The Private Life of Chairman Mao">The Private Life of Chairman Mao</a></i>, is regarded as controversial, especially by those sympathetic to Mao.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167"><span>[</span>167<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In October 1950, Mao made the decision to send the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Volunteer_Army" title="People's Volunteer Army">People's Volunteer Army</a>, a special unit of the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Liberation_Army" title="People's Liberation Army">People's Liberation Army</a>, China's armed forces into the war in Korea and fight against the United Nations forces and the South Korean armies led by the U.S. as well as to reinforce the armed forces of North Korea, the <a href="/wiki/Korean_People%27s_Army" title="Korean People's Army">Korean People's Army</a>, which had been in full retreat. Historical records showed that Mao directed the PVA campaigns in the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a> to the minute details as Chairman of the ruling CPC's Central Military Commission that oversees the country's armed forces. Because he was the Chairman of the CPC's CMC, he was also the Supreme Commander in Chief of the PLA aside from being the Chairman of the People's Republic and Chairman of the ruling CPC. The PVA was under the overall command of then newly installed Premier <a href="/wiki/Zhou_Enlai" title="Zhou Enlai">Zhou Enlai</a> and with General <a href="/wiki/Peng_Dehuai" title="Peng Dehuai">Peng Dehuai</a> as field commander and political commissar as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Burkitt_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burkitt-168"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mao_and_Jiang_Qing_1946.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Mao_and_Jiang_Qing_1946.jpg/220px-Mao_and_Jiang_Qing_1946.jpg" width="220" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Mao_and_Jiang_Qing_1946.jpg/330px-Mao_and_Jiang_Qing_1946.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Mao_and_Jiang_Qing_1946.jpg/440px-Mao_and_Jiang_Qing_1946.jpg 2x" data-file-width="609" data-file-height="708" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mao_and_Jiang_Qing_1946.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mao with his fourth wife, <a href="/wiki/Jiang_Qing" title="Jiang Qing">Jiang Qing</a>, called "Madame Mao", 1946</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Along with <a href="/wiki/Land_reforms_by_country#China" title="Land reforms by country">land reform</a>, during which significant numbers of landlords and well-to-do peasants were beaten to death at mass meetings organised by the Communist Party as land was taken from them and given to poorer peasants,<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup> there was also the <a href="/wiki/Campaign_to_Suppress_Counterrevolutionaries" title="Campaign to Suppress Counterrevolutionaries">Campaign to Suppress Counter-revolutionaries</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Yang_Kuisong_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yang_Kuisong-170"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup> which involved public executions targeting mainly former Kuomintang officials, businessmen accused of "disturbing" the market, former employees of Western companies and intellectuals whose loyalty was suspect.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171"><span>[</span>171<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">U.S. State department</a> in 1976 estimated that there may have been a million killed in the land reform, and 800,000 killed in the counter-revolutionary campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172"><span>[</span>172<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Mao himself claimed that a total of 700,000 people were killed in attacks on "counter-revolutionaries" during the years 1950–52.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup> However, because there was a policy to select "at least one landlord, and usually several, in virtually every village for public execution",<sup id="cite_ref-Cambridge_history_of_China_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge_history_of_China-174"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup> the number of deaths range between 2 million<sup id="cite_ref-Cambridge_history_of_China_174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge_history_of_China-174"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175"><span>[</span>175<span>]</span></a></sup> and 5 million.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177"><span>[</span>177<span>]</span></a></sup> In addition, at least 1.5 million people,<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup> perhaps as many as 4 to 6 million,<sup id="cite_ref-Valentino2004_121.E2.80.93122_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Valentino2004_121.E2.80.93122-179"><span>[</span>179<span>]</span></a></sup> were sent to <a href="/wiki/Laogai" title="Laogai">"reform through labour"</a> camps where many perished.<sup id="cite_ref-Valentino2004_121.E2.80.93122_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Valentino2004_121.E2.80.93122-179"><span>[</span>179<span>]</span></a></sup> Mao played a personal role in organizing the mass repressions and established a system of execution quotas,<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180"><span>[</span>180<span>]</span></a></sup> which were often exceeded.<sup id="cite_ref-Yang_Kuisong_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yang_Kuisong-170"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup> He defended these killings as necessary for the securing of power.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181"><span>[</span>181<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Starting in 1951, Mao initiated two successive movements in an effort to rid urban areas of corruption by targeting wealthy capitalists and political opponents, known as the <a href="/wiki/Three-anti/five-anti_campaigns" title="Three-anti/five-anti campaigns" class="mw-redirect">three-anti/five-anti campaigns</a>. Whereas the three-anti campaign was a focused purge of government, industrial and party officials, the five-anti campaign set its sights slightly broader, targeting capitalist elements in general.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182"><span>[</span>182<span>]</span></a></sup> Workers denounced their bosses, spouses turned on their spouses, and children informed on their parents; the victims were often humiliated at <a href="/wiki/Struggle_Session" title="Struggle Session" class="mw-redirect">struggle sessions</a>, a method designed to intimidate and terrify people to the maximum. Mao insisted that minor offenders be criticised and reformed or sent to labour camps, "while the worst among them should be shot". These campaigns took several hundred thousand additional lives, the vast majority via suicide.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In Shanghai, suicide by jumping from tall buildings became so commonplace that residents avoided walking on the pavement near skyscrapers for fear that suicides might land on them.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup> Some biographers have pointed out that driving those perceived as enemies to suicide was a common tactic during the Mao-era. For example, in his biography of Mao, <a href="/wiki/Philip_Short" title="Philip Short">Philip Short</a> notes that in the <a href="/wiki/Yan%27an_Rectification_Movement" title="Yan'an Rectification Movement">Yan'an Rectification Movement</a>, Mao gave explicit instructions that "no cadre is to be killed", but in practice allowed security chief <a href="/wiki/Kang_Sheng" title="Kang Sheng">Kang Sheng</a> to drive opponents to suicide and that "this pattern was repeated throughout his leadership of the People's Republic."<sup id="cite_ref-deathtoll_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deathtoll-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mao,_Bulganin,_Stalin,_Ulbricht_Tsedenbal.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Mao%2C_Bulganin%2C_Stalin%2C_Ulbricht_Tsedenbal.jpeg/220px-Mao%2C_Bulganin%2C_Stalin%2C_Ulbricht_Tsedenbal.jpeg" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Mao%2C_Bulganin%2C_Stalin%2C_Ulbricht_Tsedenbal.jpeg/330px-Mao%2C_Bulganin%2C_Stalin%2C_Ulbricht_Tsedenbal.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Mao%2C_Bulganin%2C_Stalin%2C_Ulbricht_Tsedenbal.jpeg/440px-Mao%2C_Bulganin%2C_Stalin%2C_Ulbricht_Tsedenbal.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="466" data-file-height="297" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mao,_Bulganin,_Stalin,_Ulbricht_Tsedenbal.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mao at <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a>'s 70th birthday celebration in Moscow, December 1949</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following the consolidation of power, Mao launched the First <a href="/wiki/Five-year_plans_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="Five-year plans of the People's Republic of China" class="mw-redirect">Five-Year Plan</a> (1953–58). The plan aimed to end Chinese dependence upon agriculture in order to become a world power. With the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>'s assistance, new industrial plants were built and agricultural production eventually fell to a point where industry was beginning to produce enough capital that China no longer needed the USSR's support. The success of the First-Five Year Plan was to encourage Mao to instigate the Second <a href="/wiki/Five-year_plans_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="Five-year plans of the People's Republic of China" class="mw-redirect">Five-Year Plan</a> in 1958. Mao also launched a phase of rapid <a href="/wiki/Collectivization" title="Collectivization" class="mw-redirect">collectivization</a>. The CPC introduced price controls as well as a <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_character" title="Simplified Chinese character" class="mw-redirect">Chinese character simplification</a> aimed at increasing literacy. Large-scale industrialization projects were also undertaken.</p>
<p>Programs pursued during this time include the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Flowers_Campaign" title="Hundred Flowers Campaign">Hundred Flowers Campaign</a>, in which Mao indicated his supposed willingness to consider different opinions about how China should be governed. Given the freedom to express themselves, liberal and intellectual Chinese began opposing the Communist Party and questioning its leadership. This was initially tolerated and encouraged. After a few months, Mao's government reversed its policy and persecuted those, totalling perhaps 500,000,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> who criticised, as well as those who were merely alleged to have criticised, the party in what is called the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Rightist_Movement" title="Anti-Rightist Movement">Anti-Rightist Movement</a>. Authors such as <a href="/wiki/Jung_Chang" title="Jung Chang">Jung Chang</a> have alleged that the Hundred Flowers Campaign was merely a ruse to root out "dangerous" thinking.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185"><span>[</span>185<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Li Zhisui, Mao's physician, suggested that Mao had initially seen the policy as a way of weakening those within his party who opposed him and was surprised by the extent of criticism and the fact that it began to be directed at his own leadership.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup> It was only then that he used it as a method of identifying and subsequently persecuting those critical of his government. The Hundred Flowers movement led to the condemnation, silencing, and death of many citizens, also linked to Mao's Anti-Rightist Movement, with death tolls possibly in the millions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Leap_Forward">Great Leap Forward</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nikita_Khrushchev,_Mao_Zedong,_Ho_Chi_Minh_and_Soong_Ching-ling.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Nikita_Khrushchev%2C_Mao_Zedong%2C_Ho_Chi_Minh_and_Soong_Ching-ling.jpg/220px-Nikita_Khrushchev%2C_Mao_Zedong%2C_Ho_Chi_Minh_and_Soong_Ching-ling.jpg" width="220" height="120" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Nikita_Khrushchev%2C_Mao_Zedong%2C_Ho_Chi_Minh_and_Soong_Ching-ling.jpg/330px-Nikita_Khrushchev%2C_Mao_Zedong%2C_Ho_Chi_Minh_and_Soong_Ching-ling.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Nikita_Khrushchev%2C_Mao_Zedong%2C_Ho_Chi_Minh_and_Soong_Ching-ling.jpg/440px-Nikita_Khrushchev%2C_Mao_Zedong%2C_Ho_Chi_Minh_and_Soong_Ching-ling.jpg 2x" data-file-width="970" data-file-height="530" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nikita_Khrushchev,_Mao_Zedong,_Ho_Chi_Minh_and_Soong_Ching-ling.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mao with <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ho_Chi_Minh" title="Ho Chi Minh">Ho Chi Minh</a> during a state dinner in Beijing, 1959</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In January 1958, Mao launched the second <a href="/wiki/Five-year_plans_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="Five-year plans of the People's Republic of China" class="mw-redirect">Five-Year Plan</a>, known as the <a href="/wiki/Great_Leap_Forward" title="Great Leap Forward">Great Leap Forward</a>, a plan intended as an alternative model for economic growth to the Soviet model focusing on heavy industry that was advocated by others in the party. Under this economic program, the relatively small agricultural collectives which had been formed to date were rapidly merged into far larger <a href="/wiki/People%27s_commune" title="People's commune">people's communes</a>, and many of the peasants were ordered to work on massive infrastructure projects and on the production of iron and steel. Some private food production was banned; livestock and farm implements were brought under collective ownership.</p>
<p>Under the Great Leap Forward, Mao and other party leaders ordered the implementation of a variety of unproven and unscientific new agricultural techniques by the new communes. Combined with the diversion of labour to steel production and infrastructure projects, these projects combined with cyclical <a href="/wiki/Natural_disasters" title="Natural disasters" class="mw-redirect">natural disasters</a> led to an approximately 15% drop in grain production in 1959 followed by a further 10% decline in 1960 and no recovery in 1961.<sup id="cite_ref-Spence1999_p553_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spence1999_p553-187"><span>[</span>187<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In an effort to win favour with their superiors and avoid being purged, each layer in the party hierarchy exaggerated the amount of grain produced under them. Based upon the fabricated success, party cadres were ordered to requisition a disproportionately high amount of the true harvest for state use, primarily in the cities and urban areas but also for export. The net result, which was compounded in some areas by drought and in others by floods, left rural peasants with little food for themselves and many millions starved to death in the largest <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a> known as the <a href="/wiki/Great_Chinese_Famine" title="Great Chinese Famine">Great Chinese Famine</a>. This famine was a direct cause of the death of some 30 million Chinese peasants between 1959 and 1962.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188"><span>[</span>188<span>]</span></a></sup> Further, many children who became emaciated and malnourished during years of hardship and struggle for survival died shortly after the Great Leap Forward came to an end in 1962.<sup id="cite_ref-Spence1999_p553_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spence1999_p553-187"><span>[</span>187<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The extent of Mao's knowledge of the severity of the situation has been disputed. Mao's physician believed that he may have been unaware of the extent of the famine, partly due to a reluctance to criticise his policies and decisions and the willingness of his staff to exaggerate or outright fake reports regarding food production.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189"><span>[</span>189<span>]</span></a></sup> Upon learning of the extent of the starvation, Mao vowed to stop eating meat, an action followed by his staff.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:People%27s_commone_canteen3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/People%27s_commone_canteen3.jpg/220px-People%27s_commone_canteen3.jpg" width="220" height="229" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/People%27s_commone_canteen3.jpg/330px-People%27s_commone_canteen3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a8/People%27s_commone_canteen3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="416" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:People%27s_commone_canteen3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In the beginning, commune members were able to eat for free at the commune canteens. This changed when food production slowed to a halt.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hong Kong-based historian <a href="/wiki/Frank_Dik%C3%B6tter" title="Frank Dikötter">Frank Dikötter</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191"><span>[</span>191<span>]</span></a></sup> challenged the notion that Mao did not know about the famine throughout the country until it was too late:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The idea that the state mistakenly took too much grain from the countryside because it assumed that the harvest was much larger than it was is largely a myth—at most partially true for the autumn of 1958 only. In most cases the party knew very well that it was starving its own people to death. At a secret meeting in the Jinjiang Hotel in Shanghai dated March 25, 1959, Mao specifically ordered the party to procure up to one third of all the grain, much more than had ever been the case. At the meeting he announced that "To distribute resources evenly will only ruin the Great Leap Forward. When there is not enough to eat, people starve to death. It is better to let half of the people die so that the other half can eat their fill."<sup id="cite_ref-Dikottersite_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dikottersite-192"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193"><span>[</span>193<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Professor Emeritus Thomas P. Bernstein of the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a> offered his view on Mao's statement on starvation in the March 25, 1959 meeting:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Some scholars believe that this shows Mao’s readiness to accept mass death on an immense scale. My own view is that this is an instance of Mao’s use of hyperbole, another being his casual acceptance of death of half the population during a nuclear war. In other contexts, Mao did not in fact accept mass death. Zhou’s Chronology shows that in October 1958, Mao expressed real concern that 40,000 people in Yunnan had starved to death (p. 173). Shortly after the March 25 meeting, he worried about 25.2 million people who were at risk of starvation.<sup id="cite_ref-wilfulness_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wilfulness-194"><span>[</span>194<span>]</span></a></sup> But from late summer on, Mao essentially forgot about this issue, until, as noted, the "Xinyang Incident" came to light in October 1960.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In the article "Mao Zedong and the Famine of 1959–1960: A Study in Wilfulness", published in 2006 in <i><a href="/wiki/The_China_Quarterly" title="The China Quarterly">The China Quarterly</a></i>, Professor Thomas P. Bernstein also discussed Mao's change of attitudes during different phases of the Great Leap Forward:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>In late autumn 1958, Mao Zedong strongly condemned widespread practices of the Great Leap Forward (GLF) such as subjecting peasants to exhausting labour without adequate food and rest, which had resulted in epidemics, starvation and deaths. At that time Mao explicitly recognized that anti-rightist pressures on officialdom were a major cause of "production at the expense of livelihood." While he was not willing to acknowledge that only abandonment of the GLF could solve these problems, he did strongly demand that they be addressed. After the July 1959 <a href="/wiki/Lushan_Conference" title="Lushan Conference">clash at Lushan</a> with <a href="/wiki/Peng_Dehuai" title="Peng Dehuai">Peng Dehuai</a>, Mao revived the GLF in the context of a new, extremely harsh anti-rightist campaign, which he relentlessly promoted into the spring of 1960 together with the radical policies that he previously condemned. Not until spring 1960 did Mao again express concern about abnormal deaths and other abuses, but he failed to apply the pressure needed to stop them. Given what he had already learned about the costs to the peasants of GLF extremism, the Chairman should have known that the revival of GLF radicalism would exact a similar or even bigger price. Instead, he wilfully ignored the lessons of the first radical phase for the sake of achieving extreme ideological and developmental goals.<sup id="cite_ref-wilfulness_194-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wilfulness-194"><span>[</span>194<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Hungry_Ghosts:_Mao%27s_Secret_Famine" title="Hungry Ghosts: Mao's Secret Famine">Hungry Ghosts: Mao's Secret Famine</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Jasper_Becker" title="Jasper Becker">Jasper Becker</a> notes that Mao was dismissive of reports he received of food shortages in the countryside and refused to change course, believing that peasants were lying and that <a href="/wiki/Rightists" title="Rightists" class="mw-redirect">rightists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kulaks" title="Kulaks" class="mw-redirect">kulaks</a> were hoarding grain. He refused to open state granaries,<sup id="cite_ref-Becker81_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Becker81-196"><span>[</span>196<span>]</span></a></sup> and instead launched a series of "anti-grain concealment" drives that resulted in numerous purges and suicides.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup> Other violent campaigns followed in which party leaders went from village to village in search of hidden food reserves, and not only grain, as Mao issued quotas for pigs, chickens, ducks and eggs. Many peasants accused of hiding food were tortured and beaten to death.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198"><span>[</span>198<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Whatever the case, the Great Leap Forward caused Mao to lose esteem among many of the top party cadres and was eventually forced to abandon the policy in 1962, while losing some political power to moderate leaders, perhaps most notably <a href="/wiki/Liu_Shaoqi" title="Liu Shaoqi">Liu Shaoqi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deng_Xiaoping" title="Deng Xiaoping">Deng Xiaoping</a> in the process. However, Mao, supported by national propaganda, claimed that he was only partly to blame for the famine in China. As a result, he was able to remain Chairman of the Communist Party, with the Presidency transferred to Liu Shaoqi.</p>
<p>The Great Leap Forward was a tragedy for the vast majority of the Chinese. Although the steel quotas were officially reached, almost all of the supposed steel made in the countryside was iron, as it had been made from assorted scrap metal in home-made furnaces with no reliable source of fuel such as coal. This meant that proper <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelting</a> conditions could not be achieved. According to Zhang Rongmei, a geometry teacher in rural Shanghai during the Great Leap Forward:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"We took all the furniture, pots, and pans we had in our house, and all our neighbours did likewise. We put everything in a big fire and melted down all the metal."</p>
</blockquote>
<p>The worst of the famine was steered towards enemies of the state.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199"><span>[</span>199<span>]</span></a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Jasper_Becker" title="Jasper Becker">Jasper Becker</a> explains:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"The most vulnerable section of China's population, around five per cent, were those whom Mao called '<a href="/wiki/Enemies_of_the_people" title="Enemies of the people" class="mw-redirect">enemies of the people</a>'. Anyone who had in previous campaigns of repression been labeled a 'black element' was given the lowest priority in the allocation of food. Landlords, rich peasants, former members of the nationalist regime, religious leaders, rightists, counter-revolutionaries and the families of such individuals died in the greatest numbers."<i><sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200"><span>[</span>200<span>]</span></a></sup></i></p>
</blockquote>
<p>At a large Communist Party conference in Beijing in January 1962, called the "Conference of the Seven Thousand", State Chairman Liu Shaoqi denounced the Great Leap Forward as responsible for widespread famine.<sup id="cite_ref-Chang_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chang-201"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup> The overwhelming majority of delegates expressed agreement, but Defense Minister <a href="/wiki/Lin_Biao" title="Lin Biao">Lin Biao</a> staunchly defended Mao.<sup id="cite_ref-Chang_201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chang-201"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup> A brief period of liberalization followed while Mao and Lin plotted a comeback.<sup id="cite_ref-Chang_201-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chang-201"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup> Liu Shaoqi and <a href="/wiki/Deng_Xiaoping" title="Deng Xiaoping">Deng Xiaoping</a> rescued the economy by disbanding the people's communes, introducing elements of private control of peasant smallholdings and importing grain from Canada and Australia to mitigate the worst effects of famine.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kissinger_Mao.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Kissinger_Mao.jpg/220px-Kissinger_Mao.jpg" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Kissinger_Mao.jpg/330px-Kissinger_Mao.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Kissinger_Mao.jpg/440px-Kissinger_Mao.jpg 2x" data-file-width="499" data-file-height="349" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kissinger_Mao.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mao with <a href="/wiki/Henry_Kissinger" title="Henry Kissinger">Henry Kissinger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zhou_Enlai" title="Zhou Enlai">Zhou Enlai</a>; Beijing, 1972.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consequences">Consequences</span></h3>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/Lushan_Conference" title="Lushan Conference">Lushan Conference</a> in July/August 1959, several ministers expressed concern that the Great Leap Forward had not proved as successful as planned. The most direct of these was Minister of Defence and <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a> veteran General <a href="/wiki/Peng_Dehuai" title="Peng Dehuai">Peng Dehuai</a>. Following Peng's criticism of the Great Leap Forward, Mao orchestrated a purge of Peng and his supporters, stifling criticism of the Great Leap policies. Senior officials who reported the truth of the famine to Mao were branded as "right opportunists."<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202"><span>[</span>202<span>]</span></a></sup> A campaign against right-wing opportunism was launched and resulted in party members and ordinary peasants being sent to prison labor camps where many would subsequently die in the famine. Years later the CPC would conclude that as many as six million people were wrongly punished in the campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203"><span>[</span>203<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The number of deaths by starvation during the Great Leap Forward is deeply controversial. Until the mid-1980s, when official census figures were finally published by the Chinese Government, little was known about the scale of the disaster in the Chinese countryside, as the handful of Western observers allowed access during this time had been restricted to model villages where they were deceived into believing that the Great Leap Forward had been a great success. There was also an assumption that the flow of individual reports of starvation that had been reaching the West, primarily through Hong Kong and Taiwan, must have been localised or exaggerated as China was continuing to claim record harvests and was a net exporter of grain through the period. Because Mao wanted to pay back early to the Soviets debts totalling 1.973 billion <a href="/wiki/Chinese_yuan" title="Chinese yuan">yuan</a> from 1960 to 1962,<sup id="cite_ref-Yang_Jisheng_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yang_Jisheng-204"><span>[</span>204<span>]</span></a></sup> exports increased by 50%, and fellow Communist regimes in <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Vietnam" title="North Vietnam">North Vietnam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Socialist_People%27s_Republic_of_Albania" title="Socialist People's Republic of Albania" class="mw-redirect">Albania</a> were provided grain free of charge.<sup id="cite_ref-Becker81_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Becker81-196"><span>[</span>196<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Censuses were carried out in China in 1953, 1964 and 1982. The first attempt to analyse this data to estimate the number of famine deaths was carried out by American demographer Dr. Judith Banister and published in 1984. Given the lengthy gaps between the censuses and doubts over the reliability of the data, an accurate figure is difficult to ascertain. Nevertheless, Banister concluded that the official data implied that around 15 million excess deaths incurred in China during 1958–61, and that based on her modelling of Chinese demographics during the period and taking account of assumed under-reporting during the famine years, the figure was around 30 million. The official statistic is 20 million deaths, as given by <a href="/wiki/Hu_Yaobang" title="Hu Yaobang">Hu Yaobang</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205"><span>[</span>205<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yang_Jisheng" title="Yang Jisheng">Yang Jisheng</a>, a former <a href="/wiki/Xinhua_News_Agency" title="Xinhua News Agency">Xinhua News Agency</a> reporter who had privileged access and connections available to no other scholars, estimates a death toll of 36 million.<sup id="cite_ref-Yang_Jisheng_204-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yang_Jisheng-204"><span>[</span>204<span>]</span></a></sup> Frank Dikötter estimates that there were at least 45 million premature deaths attributable to the Great Leap Forward from 1958 to 1962.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206"><span>[</span>206<span>]</span></a></sup> Various other sources have put the figure at between 20 and 46 million.<sup id="cite_ref-maostats_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-maostats-207"><span>[</span>207<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Split_from_Soviet_Union">Split from Soviet Union</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kissinger,_Ford_and_Mao,_1975_A7912.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Kissinger%2C_Ford_and_Mao%2C_1975_A7912.jpg/220px-Kissinger%2C_Ford_and_Mao%2C_1975_A7912.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Kissinger%2C_Ford_and_Mao%2C_1975_A7912.jpg/330px-Kissinger%2C_Ford_and_Mao%2C_1975_A7912.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Kissinger%2C_Ford_and_Mao%2C_1975_A7912.jpg/440px-Kissinger%2C_Ford_and_Mao%2C_1975_A7912.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1158" data-file-height="822" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kissinger,_Ford_and_Mao,_1975_A7912.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
US President <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a> watches as Henry Kissinger shakes hands with Mao Zedong during their visit to China, December 2, 1975</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sino-Soviet_split" title="Sino-Soviet split">Sino-Soviet split</a></div>
<p>On the international front, the period was dominated by the further isolation of China. The <a href="/wiki/Sino-Soviet_split" title="Sino-Soviet split">Sino-Soviet split</a> resulted in <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a>'s withdrawal of all Soviet technical experts and aid from the country. The split concerned the leadership of <a href="/wiki/World_Communism" title="World Communism" class="mw-redirect">world Communism</a>. The USSR had a network of Communist parties it supported; China now created its own rival network to battle it out for local control of the left in numerous countries.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208"><span>[</span>208<span>]</span></a></sup> Lorenz M. Lüthi argues:</p>
<dl>
<dd>The Sino-Soviet split was one of the key events of the Cold War, equal in importance to the construction of the Berlin Wall, the Cuban Missile Crisis, the Second Vietnam War, and Sino-American rapprochement. The split helped to determine the framework of the Second Cold War in general, and influenced the course of the Second Vietnam War in particular.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209"><span>[</span>209<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>The split resulted from <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a>'s more moderate Soviet leadership after the death of Stalin in March 1953. Only <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_Albania" title="People's Republic of Albania" class="mw-redirect">Albania</a> openly sided with China, thereby forming an alliance between the two countries which would last until after Mao's death in 1976. Warned that the Soviets had nuclear weapons, Mao minimized the threat. Becker says that, "Mao believed that the bomb was a 'paper tiger', declaring to Khrushchev that it would not matter if China lost 300 million people in a nuclear war: the other half of the population would survive to ensure victory."<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210"><span>[</span>210<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Stalin had established himself as the successor of "correct" Marxist thought well before Mao controlled the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_China" title="Communist Party of China">Communist Party of China</a>, and therefore Mao never challenged the suitability of any Stalinist doctrine (at least while Stalin was alive). Upon the death of Stalin, Mao believed (perhaps because of seniority) that the leadership of the "correct" Marxist doctrine would fall to him. The resulting tension between Khrushchev (at the head of a politically and militarily superior government), and Mao (believing he had a superior understanding of Marxist ideology) eroded the previous patron-client relationship between the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Communist Party of the Soviet Union">Communist Party of the Soviet Union</a> and the CPC.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In China, the formerly favourable Soviets were now denounced as "revisionists" and listed alongside "American imperialism" as movements to oppose.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Partly surrounded by hostile American military bases (in South Korea, Japan, and <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>), China was now confronted with a new <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> threat from the north and west. Both the internal crisis and the external threat called for extraordinary statesmanship from Mao, but as China entered the new decade the statesmen of the People's Republic were in hostile confrontation with each other.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Proletarian_Cultural_Revolution">Great Proletarian Cultural Revolution</span></h3>
<p>During the early 1960s, Mao became concerned with the nature of post-1959 China. He saw that the revolution and Great Leap Forward had replaced the old elite with a new one. He was concerned that those in power were becoming estranged from the people they were supposed to serve. Mao believed that a revolution of culture would unseat and unsettle the "ruling class" and keep China in a state of "perpetual revolution" that, theoretically, would serve the interests of the majority, not a tiny and privileged <a href="/wiki/Elite" title="Elite">elite</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211"><span>[</span>211<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Liu_Shaoqi" title="Liu Shaoqi">Liu Shaoqi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deng_Xiaoping" title="Deng Xiaoping">Deng Xiaoping</a>, then the State Chairman and General Secretary, respectively, had favoured the idea that Mao should be removed from actual power but maintain his ceremonial and symbolic role, with the party upholding all of his positive contributions to the revolution. They attempted to marginalise Mao by taking control of economic policy and asserting themselves politically as well. Many claim that Mao responded to Liu and Deng's movements by launching the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Great Proletarian Cultural Revolution</a> in 1966. Some scholars, such as Mobo Gao, claim the case for this is perhaps overstated.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212"><span>[</span>212<span>]</span></a></sup> Others, such as <a href="/wiki/Frank_Dik%C3%B6tter" title="Frank Dikötter">Frank Dikötter</a>, hold that Mao launched the Cultural Revolution to wreak revenge on those who had dared to challenge him over the Great Leap Forward.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213"><span>[</span>213<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Believing that certain liberal bourgeois elements of society continued to threaten the socialist framework, groups of young people known as the <a href="/wiki/Red_Guards_(China)" title="Red Guards (China)">Red Guards</a> struggled against authorities at all levels of society and even set up their own tribunals. Chaos reigned in much of the nation, and millions were persecuted, including a famous philosopher, Chen Yuen. During the Cultural Revolution, nearly all of the schools and universities in China were closed and the young intellectuals living in cities were ordered to the countryside to be "re-educated" by the peasants, where they performed hard manual labour and other work.</p>
<p>The Cultural Revolution led to the destruction of much of China's traditional cultural heritage and the imprisonment of a huge number of Chinese citizens, as well as creating general economic and social chaos in the country. Millions of lives were ruined during this period, as the Cultural Revolution pierced into every part of Chinese life, depicted by such Chinese films as <i><a href="/wiki/To_Live_(film)" title="To Live (film)" class="mw-redirect">To Live</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Blue_Kite" title="The Blue Kite">The Blue Kite</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Farewell_My_Concubine_(film)" title="Farewell My Concubine (film)">Farewell My Concubine</a></i>. It is estimated that hundreds of thousands, perhaps millions, perished in the violence of the Cultural Revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-maostats_207-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-maostats-207"><span>[</span>207<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>When Mao was informed of such losses, particularly that people had been driven to suicide, he is alleged to have commented: "People who try to commit suicide&#160;— don't attempt to save them!&#160;.&#160;.&#160;. China is such a populous nation, it is not as if we cannot do without a few people."<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214"><span>[</span>214<span>]</span></a></sup> The authorities allowed the Red Guards to abuse and kill opponents of the regime. Said <a href="/wiki/Xie_Fuzhi" title="Xie Fuzhi">Xie Fuzhi</a>, national police chief: "Don't say it is wrong of them to beat up bad persons: if in anger they beat someone to death, then so be it."<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215"><span>[</span>215<span>]</span></a></sup> As a result, in August and September 1966, there were a reported 1,772 people murdered by the Red Guards in Beijing alone.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216"><span>[</span>216<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>It was during this period that Mao chose <a href="/wiki/Lin_Biao" title="Lin Biao">Lin Biao</a>, who seemed to echo all of Mao's ideas, to become his successor. Lin was later officially named as Mao's successor. By 1971, however, a divide between the two men became apparent. Official history in China states that Lin was planning a military coup or an assassination attempt on Mao. Lin Biao died in a plane crash over the air space of Mongolia, presumably on his way to flee China, probably anticipating his arrest. The CPC declared that Lin was planning to depose Mao, and posthumously expelled Lin from the party. At this time, Mao lost trust in many of the top CPC figures. The highest-ranking Soviet Bloc intelligence defector, Lt. Gen. <a href="/wiki/Ion_Mihai_Pacepa" title="Ion Mihai Pacepa">Ion Mihai Pacepa</a> described his conversation with <a href="/wiki/Nicolae_Ceau%C8%99escu" title="Nicolae Ceaușescu">Nicolae Ceaușescu</a> who told him about a plot to kill Mao Zedong with the help of Lin Biao organised by the <a href="/wiki/KGB" title="KGB">KGB</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pacepa0_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pacepa0-217"><span>[</span>217<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Despite being considered a <a href="/wiki/Feminist" title="Feminist" class="mw-redirect">feminist</a> figure by some and a supporter of women's rights, documents released by the <a href="/wiki/US_Department_of_State" title="US Department of State" class="mw-redirect">US Department of State</a> in 2008 show that Mao declared women to be a "nonsense" in 1973, in conversation with Kissinger, joking that "China is a very poor country. We don't have much. What we have in excess is women... Let them go to your place. They will create disasters. That way you can lessen our burdens.",<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218"><span>[</span>218<span>]</span></a></sup> When Mao offered 10 million women, Kissinger replied by saying that Mao was "improving his offer".<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219"><span>[</span>219<span>]</span></a></sup> Mao and Kissinger then agreed that their comments on women be removed from public records, prompted by a Chinese official who feared that Mao's comments may incur public anger if released.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220"><span>[</span>220<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1969, Mao declared the Cultural Revolution to be over, although various historians in and outside of China mark the end of the Cultural Revolution – as a whole or in part – in 1976, following the demise of Chairman Mao Zedong.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221"><span>[</span>221<span>]</span></a></sup> In the last years of his life, Mao was faced with declining health due to either <a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s_disease" title="Parkinson's disease">Parkinson's disease</a><sup id="cite_ref-Parkinsons_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parkinsons-222"><span>[</span>222<span>]</span></a></sup> or, according to his physician, <a href="/wiki/Amyotrophic_lateral_sclerosis" title="Amyotrophic lateral sclerosis">amyotrophic lateral sclerosis</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-LouGehring_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LouGehring-223"><span>[</span>223<span>]</span></a></sup> as well as lung ailments due to smoking and heart trouble.<sup id="cite_ref-KissengerTrans_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KissengerTrans-224"><span>[</span>224<span>]</span></a></sup> Some also attributed Mao's decline in health to the betrayal of Lin Biao. Mao remained passive as various factions within the Communist Party mobilised for the power struggle anticipated after his death.</p>
<p>This period is often looked at in official circles in China and in the West as a great stagnation or even of reversal for China. While many people—an estimated 100 million—did suffer,<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225"><span>[</span>225<span>]</span></a></sup> some scholars, such as Lee Feigon and Mobo Gao, claim there were many great advances, and in some sectors the Chinese economy continued to outperform the west.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-226"><span>[</span>226<span>]</span></a></sup> They hold that the Cultural Revolution period laid the foundation for the spectacular growth that continues in China. During the Cultural Revolution, China exploded its <a href="/wiki/Test_No._6" title="Test No. 6">first H-Bomb</a> (1967), launched the <a href="/wiki/Dong_Fang_Hong" title="Dong Fang Hong" class="mw-redirect">Dong Fang Hong</a> satellite (January 30, 1970), commissioned its first nuclear submarines and made various advances in science and technology. Healthcare was free, and living standards in the countryside continued to improve.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_226-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-226"><span>[</span>226<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="State_visits">State visits</span></h2>
<table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">

</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">references or sources</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help improve this section by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. </table>

</tr>
