</tr>
</table>
<p>Mao only traveled outside of China twice during his leadership, which were two state visits to the Soviet Union. When Mao stepped down as head of state in April 1959, further state visits and travels abroad were undertaken by president <a href="/wiki/Liu_Shaoqi" title="Liu Shaoqi">Liu Shaoqi</a> rather than Mao personally.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_aftermath">Death and aftermath</span></h2>
<table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-style ambox-Tone" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" /></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section's <b>tone or style may not reflect the <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:TONE" title="Wikipedia:TONE" class="mw-redirect">encyclopedic tone</a> used on Wikipedia</b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">See Wikipedia's <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Tone" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles">guide to writing better articles</a> for suggestions.</span> <small><i>(October 2014)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum_of_Mao_Zedong" title="Mausoleum of Mao Zedong">Mausoleum of Mao Zedong</a></div>
<p>Mao was a heavy smoker<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227"><span>[</span>227<span>]</span></a></sup> during most of his adult life, and it became a <a href="/wiki/Classified_information" title="Classified information">state secret</a> that he suffered from multiple lung and heart ailments during his later years.<sup id="cite_ref-KissengerTrans_224-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KissengerTrans-224"><span>[</span>224<span>]</span></a></sup> There are unconfirmed reports that he possibly had <a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s_disease" title="Parkinson's disease">Parkinson's disease</a><sup id="cite_ref-Parkinsons_222-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parkinsons-222"><span>[</span>222<span>]</span></a></sup> in addition to <a href="/wiki/Amyotrophic_lateral_sclerosis" title="Amyotrophic lateral sclerosis">amyotrophic lateral sclerosis</a>, also known as Lou Gehrig's disease.<sup id="cite_ref-LouGehring_223-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LouGehring-223"><span>[</span>223<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Mao's last public appearance—and the last known photograph of him alive—was on May 27, 1976, when he met the visiting Pakistani Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Zulfikar_Ali_Bhutto" title="Zulfikar Ali Bhutto">Zulfikar Ali Bhutto</a> during the latter's one-day visit to Beijing.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-141"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> Mao suffered two major heart attacks in 1976, one in March and another in July, before a third struck on September 5, rendering him an invalid. Mao Zedong died nearly four days later just after midnight, at 00:10, on September 9, 1976, at age 82. The Communist Party of China delayed the announcement of his death until 16:00 later that day, when a radio message broadcast across the nation announced the news of Mao's passing while appealing for party unity.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT1_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT1-228"><span>[</span>228<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Mao's embalmed, CPC-flag-draped body lay in state at the <a href="/wiki/Great_Hall_of_the_People" title="Great Hall of the People">Great Hall of the People</a> for one week.<sup id="cite_ref-Mummy1_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mummy1-229"><span>[</span>229<span>]</span></a></sup> During this period, one million people (none of them foreign diplomats,<sup id="cite_ref-Mummy1_229-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mummy1-229"><span>[</span>229<span>]</span></a></sup> and the majority crying openly or otherwise displaying some kind of sadness)<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230"><span>[</span>230<span>]</span></a></sup> and many foreign dignitaries (300,000–750,000) filed past Mao to pay their final respects. Chairman Mao's official portrait was hung on the wall, with a banner reading: "Carry on the cause left by Chairman Mao and carry on the cause of proletarian revolution to the end", until September 17.<sup id="cite_ref-Mummy1_229-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mummy1-229"><span>[</span>229<span>]</span></a></sup> On September 17, Chairman Mao's body was taken in a minibus from the Great Hall of the people to Maojiawan to the 305 Hospital that Liu Zhisui directed, and Mao's internal organs were preserved in <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mummy1_229-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mummy1-229"><span>[</span>229<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On September 18, a somber cacophony of guns, sirens, whistles and horns all across China was spontaneously blown in observance of a three-minute silence, which everybody except those performing essential tasks was ordered to observe.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231"><span>[</span>231<span>]</span></a></sup> After that, a band in <a href="/wiki/Tiananmen_Square" title="Tiananmen Square">Tiananmen Square</a>, packed with and surrounded by millions of people, played "<a href="/wiki/The_Internationale" title="The Internationale">The Internationale</a>". The final service on that day was concluded by Hua Guofeng's 20-minute-long eulogy atop Tiananmen Gate.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232"><span>[</span>232<span>]</span></a></sup> Mao's body was later permanently interred in <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum_of_Mao_Zedong" title="Mausoleum of Mao Zedong">a mausoleum</a> in Beijing.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233"><span>[</span>233<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tiananmen_Mao.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Tiananmen_Mao.jpg/220px-Tiananmen_Mao.jpg" width="220" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Tiananmen_Mao.jpg/330px-Tiananmen_Mao.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Tiananmen_Mao.jpg/440px-Tiananmen_Mao.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1092" data-file-height="1277" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tiananmen_Mao.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A large portrait of Mao by <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Zhenshi" title="Zhang Zhenshi">Zhang Zhenshi</a> at <a href="/wiki/Tiananmen" title="Tiananmen">Tiananmen</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: left; clear: left; margin: 0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0; width:25em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: ivory;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"Had Mao died in 1956, his achievements would have been immortal. Had he died in 1966, he would still have been a great man but flawed. But he died in 1976. Alas, what can one say?"</p>
</div>
</div>
<div style="text-align: left;"><cite>—<a href="/wiki/Chen_Yun" title="Chen Yun">Chen Yun</a>, a leading Communist Party official under Mao and <a href="/wiki/Deng_Xiaoping" title="Deng Xiaoping">Deng Xiaoping</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234"><span>[</span>234<span>]</span></a></sup></cite></div>
</div>
<p>Mao remains a controversial figure and there is little agreement over his legacy both in China and abroad. Supporters generally credit him with and praise him for having unified China and for ending the previous decades of civil war. He is also credited for having improved the status of women in China and for improving literacy and education. His policies caused the deaths of tens of millions of people during his 27-year reign, more than any other Twentieth Century leader, however supporters point out that in spite of this, life expectancy improved during his reign. His supporters claim that he rapidly industrialised China; however, others have claimed that his policies such as the "Great Leap Forward" and the "Great Proletarian Cultural Revolution", were impediments to industrialisation and modernisation. His supporters claim that his policies laid the groundwork for China's later rise to become an economic superpower, while others claim that his policies delayed economic development and that China's economy only underwent its rapid growth after Mao's policies had been widely abandoned. Mao's revolutionary tactics continue to be used by insurgents, and his political ideology continues to be embraced by many Communist organizations around the world.</p>
<p>In mainland China, Mao is still revered by many supporters of the Communist Party and respected by the majority of the general population as the "<a href="/wiki/List_of_national_founders" title="List of national founders">Founding Father</a> of modern China", credited for giving "the Chinese people dignity and self-respect."<sup id="cite_ref-Biography_2005_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biography_2005-235"><span>[</span>235<span>]</span></a></sup> Mobo Gao in his 2008 book <i>The Battle for China's Past: Mao and the Cultural Revolution</i>, credits Mao for raising the average life expectancy from 35 in 1949 to 63 by 1975, bringing "unity and stability to a country that had been plagued by civil wars and foreign invasions", and laying the foundation for China to "become the equal of the great global powers".<sup id="cite_ref-MoboGao_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MoboGao-236"><span>[</span>236<span>]</span></a></sup> Gao also lauds Mao for carrying out massive <a href="/wiki/Land_reform" title="Land reform">land reform</a>, promoting the status of women, improving popular literacy, and positively "transform(ing) Chinese society beyond recognition."<sup id="cite_ref-MoboGao_236-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MoboGao-236"><span>[</span>236<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>However, Mao has many Chinese critics, both those who live inside and outside China. Opposition to Mao is subject to restriction and censorship in mainland China, but is especially strong elsewhere, where he is often reviled as a brutish ideologue. In the West, his name is generally associated with tyranny and his economic theories are widely discredited—though to some political activists he remains a symbol against <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialism</a> and western influence. Even in China, key pillars of his economic theory have been largely dismantled by market reformers like <a href="/wiki/Deng_Xiaoping" title="Deng Xiaoping">Deng Xiaoping</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zhao_Ziyang" title="Zhao Ziyang">Zhao Ziyang</a>, who succeeded him as leaders of the Communist Party.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mao_Zedong_youth_art_sculpture_4.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Mao_Zedong_youth_art_sculpture_4.jpg/170px-Mao_Zedong_youth_art_sculpture_4.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Mao_Zedong_youth_art_sculpture_4.jpg/255px-Mao_Zedong_youth_art_sculpture_4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Mao_Zedong_youth_art_sculpture_4.jpg/340px-Mao_Zedong_youth_art_sculpture_4.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="4000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mao_Zedong_youth_art_sculpture_4.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Statue of young Mao in <a href="/wiki/Changsha" title="Changsha">Changsha</a>, the capital of <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">Hunan</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Though the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Communist_Party" title="Chinese Communist Party" class="mw-redirect">Chinese Communist Party</a>, which Mao led to power, has rejected in practice the economic fundamentals of much of Mao's ideology, it retains for itself many of the powers established under Mao's reign: it controls the Chinese army, police, courts and media and does not permit multi-party elections at the national or local level, except in Hong Kong. Thus it is difficult to gauge the true extent of support for the Chinese Communist Party and Mao's legacy within mainland China. For its part, the Chinese government continues to officially regard Mao as a national hero. In 2008, China opened the Mao Zedong Square to visitors in his home town of central Hunan Province to mark the 115th anniversary of his birth.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237"><span>[</span>237<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>There continue to be disagreements on Mao's legacy. Former Party official Su Shachi, has opined that "he was a great historical criminal, but he was also a great force for good."<sup id="cite_ref-Biography_2005_235-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biography_2005-235"><span>[</span>235<span>]</span></a></sup> In a similar vein, journalist <a href="/wiki/Liu_Binyan" title="Liu Binyan">Liu Binyan</a> has described Mao as "both monster and a genius."<sup id="cite_ref-Biography_2005_235-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biography_2005-235"><span>[</span>235<span>]</span></a></sup> Some historians argue that Mao Zedong was "one of the great tyrants of the twentieth century", and a dictator comparable to <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-tyrant_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tyrant-238"><span>[</span>238<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-compare_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-compare-239"><span>[</span>239<span>]</span></a></sup> with a death toll surpassing both.<sup id="cite_ref-deathtoll_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deathtoll-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fenby_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fenby-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Black_Book_of_Communism" title="The Black Book of Communism">The Black Book of Communism</a></i>, Jean Louis Margolin writes that "Mao Zedong was so powerful that he was often known as the Red Emperor&#160;... the violence he erected into a whole system far exceeds any national tradition of violence that we might find in China."<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240"><span>[</span>240<span>]</span></a></sup> Mao was frequently likened to China's First Emperor <a href="/wiki/Qin_Shi_Huang" title="Qin Shi Huang">Qin Shi Huang</a>, notorious for <a href="/wiki/Burning_of_books_and_burying_of_scholars" title="Burning of books and burying of scholars">burying alive hundreds of scholars</a>, and personally enjoyed the comparison.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241"><span>[</span>241<span>]</span></a></sup> During a speech to party cadre in 1958, Mao said he had far outdone Qin Shi Huang in his policy against intellectuals: "He buried 460 scholars alive; we have buried forty-six thousand scholars alive&#160;... You [intellectuals] revile us for being Qin Shi Huangs. You are wrong. We have surpassed Qin Shi Huang a hundredfold."<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242"><span>[</span>242<span>]</span></a></sup> As a result of such tactics, critics have pointed out that:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The People's Republic of China under Mao exhibited the oppressive tendencies that were discernible in all the major absolutist regimes of the twentieth century. There are obvious parallels between Mao's China, <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Union_of_Soviet_Socialist_Republics" title="Union of Soviet Socialist Republics" class="mw-redirect">Soviet Russia</a>. Each of these regimes witnessed deliberately ordered mass 'cleansing' and extermination.<sup id="cite_ref-compare_239-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-compare-239"><span>[</span>239<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Others, such as <a href="/wiki/Philip_Short" title="Philip Short">Philip Short</a>, reject such comparisons in <i>Mao: A Life</i>, arguing that whereas the deaths caused by Nazi Germany and Soviet Russia were largely systematic and deliberate, the overwhelming majority of the deaths under Mao were unintended consequences of famine.<sup id="cite_ref-short_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-short-243"><span>[</span>243<span>]</span></a></sup> Short noted that landlord class were not exterminated as a people due to Mao's belief in redemption through thought reform.<sup id="cite_ref-short_243-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-short-243"><span>[</span>243<span>]</span></a></sup> He instead compared Mao with 19th-century Chinese reformers who challenged China's traditional beliefs in the era of China's clashes with Western colonial powers. Short argues, "Mao's tragedy and his grandeur were that he remained to the end in thrall to his own revolutionary dreams&#160;... He freed China from the straitjacket of its Confucian past, but the bright Red future he promised turned out to be a sterile purgatory.<sup id="cite_ref-short_243-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-short-243"><span>[</span>243<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Mao's English interpreter <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Rittenberg" title="Sidney Rittenberg">Sidney Rittenberg</a> wrote in his memoir <i>The Man Who Stayed Behind</i> that whilst Mao "was a great leader in history", he was also "a great criminal because, not that he wanted to, not that he intended to, but in fact, his wild fantasies led to the deaths of tens of millions of people."<sup id="cite_ref-Reut09_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reut09-244"><span>[</span>244<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Li_Rui_(Communist_Party_of_China)" title="Li Rui (Communist Party of China)" class="mw-redirect">Li Rui</a>, Mao's personal secretary, goes further and claims he was dismissive of the suffering and death caused by his policies: "Mao's way of thinking and governing was terrifying. He put no value on human life. The deaths of others meant nothing to him."<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245"><span>[</span>245<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mausoleo_de_Mao_Zedong-Tianang_Mei-Pekin-China8452.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Mausoleo_de_Mao_Zedong-Tianang_Mei-Pekin-China8452.JPG/220px-Mausoleo_de_Mao_Zedong-Tianang_Mei-Pekin-China8452.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Mausoleo_de_Mao_Zedong-Tianang_Mei-Pekin-China8452.JPG/330px-Mausoleo_de_Mao_Zedong-Tianang_Mei-Pekin-China8452.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Mausoleo_de_Mao_Zedong-Tianang_Mei-Pekin-China8452.JPG/440px-Mausoleo_de_Mao_Zedong-Tianang_Mei-Pekin-China8452.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mausoleo_de_Mao_Zedong-Tianang_Mei-Pekin-China8452.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sculptures in front of the Mausoleum of Mao Zedong, <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In their 832-page biography, <i><a href="/wiki/Mao:_The_Unknown_Story" title="Mao: The Unknown Story">Mao: The Unknown Story</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Jung_Chang" title="Jung Chang">Jung Chang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jon_Halliday" title="Jon Halliday">Jon Halliday</a> take a very critical view of Mao's life and influence. For example, they note that Mao was well aware that his policies would be responsible for the deaths of millions; While discussing labour-intensive projects such as waterworks and making steel, Mao said to his inner circle in November 1958: "Working like this, with all these projects, half of China may well have to die. If not half, one-third, or one-tenth—50 million—die."<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246"><span>[</span>246<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Thomas Bernstein of <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a> argues that this quotation is taken out of context, claiming:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The Chinese original, however, is not quite as shocking. In the speech, Mao talks about massive earthmoving irrigation projects and numerous big industrial ones, all requiring huge numbers of people. If the projects, he said, are all undertaken simultaneously "half of China's population unquestionably will die; and if it's not half, it'll be a third or ten percent, a death toll of 50 million people." Mao then pointed to the example of Guangxi provincial Party secretary, Chén Mànyuǎn (陈漫远) who had been dismissed in 1957 for failing to prevent famine in the previous year, adding: "If with a death toll of 50 million you didn't lose your jobs, I at least should lose mine; whether I should lose my head would also be in question. Anhui wants to do so much, which is quite all right, but make it a principle to have no deaths."<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247"><span>[</span>247<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jasper_Becker" title="Jasper Becker">Jasper Becker</a> notes, "archive material gathered by Dikötter&#160;... confirms that far from being ignorant or misled about the famine, the Chinese leadership were kept informed about it all the time. And he exposes the extent of the violence used against the peasants":<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248"><span>[</span>248<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<blockquote>
<div class="poem">
<p>Mass killings are not usually associated with Mao and the Great Leap Forward, and China continues to benefit from a more favourable comparison with Cambodia or the Soviet Union. But as fresh and abundant archival evidence shows, coercion, terror and systematic violence were the foundation of the Great Leap, and between 1958 to 1962, by a rough approximation, some 6 to 8 per cent of those who died were tortured to death or summarily killed—amounting to at least 3 million victims.<br />
<br />
Countless others were deliberately deprived of food and consequently starved to death. Many more vanished because they were too old, weak or sick to work—and hence unable to earn their keep. People were killed selectively because they had the wrong class background, because they dragged their feet, because they spoke out or simply because they were not liked, for whatever reason, by the man who wielded the ladle in the canteen.</p>
</div>
</blockquote>
<p>Dikötter argues that CPC leaders "glorified violence and were inured to massive loss of life. And all of them shared an ideology in which the end justified the means. In 1962, having lost millions of people in his province, <a href="/wiki/Li_Jingquan" title="Li Jingquan">Li Jingquan</a> compared the Great Leap Forward to the <a href="/wiki/Long_March" title="Long March">Long March</a> in which only one in ten had made it to the end: 'We are not weak, we are stronger, we have kept the backbone.'"<sup id="cite_ref-Dikotter299_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dikotter299-249"><span>[</span>249<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Regarding the large-scale irrigation projects, Dikötter stresses that, in spite of Mao being in a good position to see the human cost, they continued unabated for several years, and ultimately claimed the lives of hundreds of thousands of exhausted villagers. He also notes that "In a chilling precursor of Cambodia under the Khmer Rouge, villagers in Qingshui and Gansu called these projects the 'killing fields'."<sup id="cite_ref-Dikotter33_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dikotter33-250"><span>[</span>250<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nixon_Mao_1972-02-29.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Nixon_Mao_1972-02-29.png/220px-Nixon_Mao_1972-02-29.png" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Nixon_Mao_1972-02-29.png/330px-Nixon_Mao_1972-02-29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Nixon_Mao_1972-02-29.png/440px-Nixon_Mao_1972-02-29.png 2x" data-file-width="590" data-file-height="457" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nixon_Mao_1972-02-29.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mao greets United States President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> during his <a href="/wiki/1972_Nixon_visit_to_China" title="1972 Nixon visit to China">visit to China in 1972</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The United States placed a trade embargo on the People's Republic as a result of its involvement in the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>, lasting until <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> decided that developing relations with the PRC would be useful in dealing with the Soviet Union.</p>
<p>The television series <a href="/wiki/Biography_(TV_series)" title="Biography (TV series)">Biography</a> stated: "[Mao] turned China from a feudal backwater into one of the most powerful countries in the World&#160;... The Chinese system he overthrew was backward and corrupt; few would argue the fact that he dragged China into the 20th century. But at a cost in human lives that is staggering."<sup id="cite_ref-Biography_2005_235-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biography_2005-235"><span>[</span>235<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the book <i>China in the 21st Century: What Everyone Needs to Know</i> published in 2010, Professor <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Wasserstrom" title="Jeffrey Wasserstrom">Jeffrey N. Wasserstrom</a> of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Irvine" title="University of California, Irvine">University of California, Irvine</a> compares China’s relationship to Mao Zedong to American’s remembrance of <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>: both countries regard the leaders in a positive light, despite their respective roles in devastating policies. Jackson <a href="/wiki/Trail_of_Tears" title="Trail of Tears">forcibly moved Native Americans</a>, resulting in thousands of deaths, while Mao was at the helm during the violent years of the Cultural Revolution and the Great Leap Forward:<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251"><span>[</span>251<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<blockquote>
<p>Though admittedly far from perfect, the comparison is based on the fact that Jackson is remembered both as someone who played a significant role in the development of a political organization (the Democratic Party) that still has many partisans, and as someone responsible for brutal policies toward Native Americans that are now referred to as genocidal.</p>
<p>Both men are thought of as having done terrible things yet this does not necessarily prevent them from being used as positive symbols. And Jackson still appears on $20 bills, even though Americans tend to view as heinous the institution of slavery (of which he was a passionate defender) and the early 19th-century military campaigns against Native Americans (in which he took part).</p>
<p>At times Jackson, for all his flaws, is invoked as representing an egalitarian strain within the American democratic tradition, a self-made man of the people who rose to power via straight talk and was not allied with moneyed interests. Mao stands for something roughly similar.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252"><span>[</span>252<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Mao's military writings continue to have a large amount of influence both among those who seek to create an insurgency and those who seek to crush one, especially in manners of guerilla warfare, at which Mao is popularly regarded as a genius.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> As an example, the <a href="/wiki/Unified_Communist_Party_of_Nepal_(Maoist)" title="Unified Communist Party of Nepal (Maoist)">Communist Party of Nepal (Maoist)</a> followed Mao's examples of guerilla warfare to considerable political and military success even in the 21st century.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Mao's major contribution to the military science is his theory of <a href="/wiki/People%27s_War" title="People's War" class="mw-redirect">People's War</a>, with not only guerrilla warfare but more importantly, <a href="/wiki/Mobile_Warfare" title="Mobile Warfare">Mobile Warfare</a> methodologies. Mao had successfully applied Mobile Warfare in the Korean War, and was able to encircle, push back and then halt the UN forces in Korea, despite the clear superiority of UN firepower.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Mao also gave the impression that he might even welcome a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_war" title="Nuclear war" class="mw-redirect">nuclear war</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253"><span>[</span>253<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MaoStatueinLijang.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/MaoStatueinLijang.jpg/170px-MaoStatueinLijang.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/MaoStatueinLijang.jpg/255px-MaoStatueinLijang.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/MaoStatueinLijang.jpg/340px-MaoStatueinLijang.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2352" data-file-height="3136" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MaoStatueinLijang.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Statue of Mao in <a href="/wiki/Lijiang,_Yunnan" title="Lijiang, Yunnan" class="mw-redirect">Lijiang</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<blockquote>
<p>"Let us imagine how many people would die if war breaks out. There are 2.7 billion people in the world, and a third could be lost. If it is a little higher, it could be half&#160;... I say that if the worst came to the worst and one-half dies, there will still be one-half left, but imperialism would be razed to the ground and the whole world would become socialist. After a few years there would be 2.7 billion people again"<sup id="cite_ref-Dikotter13_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dikotter13-254"><span>[</span>254<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>But historians dispute the sincerity of Mao's words. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Service_(historian)" title="Robert Service (historian)">Robert Service</a> says that Mao "was deadly serious,"<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255"><span>[</span>255<span>]</span></a></sup> while Frank Dikötter claims that "He was bluffing&#160;... the sabre-rattling was to show that he, not Khrushchev, was the more determined revolutionary."<sup id="cite_ref-Dikotter13_254-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dikotter13-254"><span>[</span>254<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Mao's poems and writings are frequently cited by both Chinese and non-Chinese. The official Chinese translation of President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>'s inauguration speech used a famous line from one of Mao's poems.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256"><span>[</span>256<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> senator <a href="/wiki/John_McCain" title="John McCain">John McCain</a> misattributed a campaign quote to Mao several times during his 2008 presidential election bid, saying "Remember the words of Chairman Mao: 'It's always darkest before it's totally black.'"</p>
<p>The ideology of Maoism has influenced many Communists, mainly in the <a href="/wiki/Third_World" title="Third World">Third World</a>, including revolutionary movements such as <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Khmer_Rouge" title="Khmer Rouge">Khmer Rouge</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257"><span>[</span>257<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Shining_Path" title="Shining Path">Shining Path</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Nepal_(Maoist)" title="Communist Party of Nepal (Maoist)" class="mw-redirect">Nepalese revolutionary movement</a>. Under the influence of Mao's agrarian socialism and <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Cultural Revolution</a>, Cambodia's <a href="/wiki/Pol_Pot" title="Pol Pot">Pol Pot</a> conceived of his disastrous <a href="/wiki/Year_Zero_(political_notion)" title="Year Zero (political notion)">Year Zero</a> policies which purged the nation of its teachers, artists and intellectuals and emptied its cities, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Cambodian_Genocide" title="Cambodian Genocide" class="mw-redirect">Cambodian Genocide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258"><span>[</span>258<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_Communist_Party,_USA" title="Revolutionary Communist Party, USA">Revolutionary Communist Party, USA</a> also claims Marxism–Leninism-Maoism as its ideology, as do other Communist Parties around the world which are part of the <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_Internationalist_Movement" title="Revolutionary Internationalist Movement">Revolutionary Internationalist Movement</a>. China itself has moved sharply away from Maoism since Mao's death, and most people outside of China who describe themselves as Maoist regard the Deng Xiaoping reforms to be a betrayal of Maoism, in line with Mao's view of "<a href="/wiki/Capitalist_roader" title="Capitalist roader">Capitalist roaders</a>" within the Communist Party.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>As the Chinese government instituted free market economic reforms starting in the late 1970s and as later Chinese leaders took power, less recognition was given to the status of Mao. This accompanied a decline in state recognition of Mao in later years in contrast to previous years when the state organised numerous events and seminars commemorating Mao's 100th birthday. Nevertheless, the Chinese government has never officially repudiated the tactics of Mao. Deng Xiaoping, who was opposed to the Great Leap Forward and the Cultural Revolution, has to a certain extent rejected Mao's legacy, famously saying that Mao was "70% right and 30% wrong".</p>
<p>In the mid-1990s, Mao Zedong's picture began to appear on all new <a href="/wiki/Renminbi" title="Renminbi">renminbi</a> (人民幣) currency from the People's Republic of China. This was officially instituted as an anti-counterfeiting measure as Mao's face is widely recognised in contrast to the generic figures that appear in older currency. On March 13, 2006, a story in the <i><a href="/wiki/People%27s_Daily" title="People's Daily">People's Daily</a></i> reported that a proposal had been made to print the portraits of <a href="/wiki/Sun_Yat-sen" title="Sun Yat-sen">Sun Yat-sen</a> and Deng Xiaoping.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259"><span>[</span>259<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 2006, the government in Shanghai issued a new set of high school history textbooks which omit Mao, with the exception of a single mention in a section on etiquette. Students in Shanghai now only learn about Mao in junior high school.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260"><span>[</span>260<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_image">Public image</span></h3>
<p>Mao gave contradicting statements on the subject of <a href="/wiki/Cult_of_personality" title="Cult of personality">personality cults</a>. In 1955, as a response to the <a href="/wiki/Khrushchev_Report" title="Khrushchev Report" class="mw-redirect">Khrushchev Report</a> that criticised <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a>, Mao stated that personality cults are "poisonous ideological survivals of the old society", and reaffirmed China's commitment to <a href="/wiki/Collective_leadership" title="Collective leadership">collective leadership</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261"><span>[</span>261<span>]</span></a></sup> But at the 1958 Party congress in Chengdu, Mao expressed support for the personality cults of people whom he labelled as genuinely worthy figures; not those that expressed "blind worship".<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262"><span>[</span>262<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1962, Mao proposed the Socialist Education Movement (SEM) in an attempt to educate the peasants to resist the "temptations" of feudalism and the sprouts of capitalism that he saw re-emerging in the countryside from Liu's economic reforms.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Large quantities of politicised art were produced and circulated&#160;— with Mao at the centre. Numerous posters, <a href="/wiki/Chairman_Mao_badge" title="Chairman Mao badge">badges</a> and musical compositions referenced Mao in the phrase "Chairman Mao is the red sun in our hearts" (<span lang="zh">毛主席是我们心中的红太阳</span>, <i>Máo Zhǔxí Shì Wǒmen Xīnzhōng De Hóng Tàiyáng</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-WangMaoBadgesChapter5_263-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WangMaoBadgesChapter5-263"><span>[</span>263<span>]</span></a></sup> and a "Savior of the people" (<span lang="zh">人民的大救星</span>, <i>Rénmín De Dà Jiùxīng</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-WangMaoBadgesChapter5_263-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WangMaoBadgesChapter5-263"><span>[</span>263<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In October 1966, Mao's <i><a href="/wiki/Quotations_from_Chairman_Mao_Tse-tung" title="Quotations from Chairman Mao Tse-tung">Quotations from Chairman Mao Tse-tung</a></i>, which was known as the <i>Little Red Book</i> was published. Party members were encouraged to carry a copy with them and possession was almost mandatory as a criterion for membership. Over the years, Mao's image became displayed almost everywhere, present in homes, offices and shops. His quotations were <a href="/wiki/Emphasis_(typography)" title="Emphasis (typography)">typographically emphasised</a> by putting them in boldface or red type in even the most obscure writings. Music from the period emphasised Mao's stature, as did children's rhymes. The phrase "Long Live Chairman Mao for <a href="/wiki/Ten_thousand_years" title="Ten thousand years">ten thousand years</a>" was commonly heard during the era.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264"><span>[</span>264<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:552px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mao_mausoleum_queue.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Mao_mausoleum_queue.jpg/550px-Mao_mausoleum_queue.jpg" width="550" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Mao_mausoleum_queue.jpg/825px-Mao_mausoleum_queue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Mao_mausoleum_queue.jpg/1100px-Mao_mausoleum_queue.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6548" data-file-height="1638" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mao_mausoleum_queue.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A line to enter Mao Zedong Mausoleum</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Mao also has a presence in China and around the world in popular culture, where his face adorns everything from T-shirts to coffee cups. Mao's granddaughter, Kong Dongmei, defended the phenomenon, stating that "it shows his influence, that he exists in people's consciousness and has influenced several generations of Chinese people's way of life. Just like <a href="/wiki/Che_Guevara_in_popular_culture" title="Che Guevara in popular culture">Che Guevara's image</a>, his has become a symbol of revolutionary culture."<sup id="cite_ref-Reut09_244-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reut09-244"><span>[</span>244<span>]</span></a></sup> Since 1950, over 40 million people have visited Mao's birthplace in <a href="/wiki/Shaoshan" title="Shaoshan">Shaoshan</a>, Hunan.<sup id="cite_ref-ShaoShan_265-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ShaoShan-265"><span>[</span>265<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genealogy">Genealogy</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancestors">Ancestors</span></h3>
<p>His ancestors were:</p>
<ul>
<li>Máo Yíchāng (毛贻昌, born <a href="/wiki/Xiangtan" title="Xiangtan">Xiangtan</a> October 15, 1870, died <a href="/wiki/Shaoshan" title="Shaoshan">Shaoshan</a> January 23, 1920), father, <a href="/wiki/Courtesy_name" title="Courtesy name">courtesy name</a> Máo Shùnshēng (毛顺生) or also known as Mao Jen-sheng</li>
<li>Wén Qīmèi(文七妹, born Xiangxiang 1867, died October 5, 1919), mother. She was illiterate and a devout Buddhist. She was a descendant of <a href="/wiki/Wen_Tianxiang" title="Wen Tianxiang">Wen Tianxiang</a>.</li>
<li>Máo Ēnpǔ (毛恩普, born May 22, 1846, died November 23, 1904), paternal grandfather</li>
<li>née Luó (罗氏), paternal grandmother (given name not recorded)<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266"><span>[</span>266<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Máo Zǔrén (毛祖人), paternal great-grandfather</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wives">Wives</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mao_Jiang_Qing_and_daughter_Li_Na.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Mao_Jiang_Qing_and_daughter_Li_Na.jpg/220px-Mao_Jiang_Qing_and_daughter_Li_Na.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Mao_Jiang_Qing_and_daughter_Li_Na.jpg/330px-Mao_Jiang_Qing_and_daughter_Li_Na.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Mao_Jiang_Qing_and_daughter_Li_Na.jpg/440px-Mao_Jiang_Qing_and_daughter_Li_Na.jpg 2x" data-file-width="709" data-file-height="469" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mao_Jiang_Qing_and_daughter_Li_Na.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mao with <a href="/wiki/Jiang_Qing" title="Jiang Qing">Jiang Qing</a> and daughter <a href="/wiki/Li_Na_(daughter_of_Mao_Zedong)" title="Li Na (daughter of Mao Zedong)">Li Na</a>, 1940s</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Mao Zedong had four wives who gave birth to a total of 10 children. These were:</p>
<ol>
<li><a href="/wiki/Luo_Yixiu" title="Luo Yixiu">Luo Yixiu</a> (罗一秀, October 20, 1889 – 1910) of <a href="/wiki/Shaoshan" title="Shaoshan">Shaoshan</a>: married 1907 to 1910</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Yang_Kaihui" title="Yang Kaihui">Yang Kaihui</a> (杨开慧, 1901–1930) of <a href="/wiki/Changsha" title="Changsha">Changsha</a>: married 1921 to 1927, executed by the KMT in 1930; mother to <a href="/wiki/Mao_Anying" title="Mao Anying">Mao Anying</a>, Mao Anqing, and Mao Anlong</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/He_Zizhen" title="He Zizhen">He Zizhen</a> (贺子珍, 1910–1984) of Jiangxi: married May 1928 to 1939; mother to Mao Anhong, <a href="/wiki/Li_Min_(politician)" title="Li Min (politician)">Li Min</a>, and four other children</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jiang_Qing" title="Jiang Qing">Jiang Qing</a> (江青, 1914–1991), married 1939 to Mao's death; mother to <a href="/wiki/Li_Na_(daughter_of_Mao_Zedong)" title="Li Na (daughter of Mao Zedong)">Li Na</a></li>
</ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Siblings">Siblings</span></h3>
<p>He had several siblings:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mao_Zemin" title="Mao Zemin">Mao Zemin</a> (毛泽民, 1895–1943), younger brother, executed by a warlord</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mao_Zetan" title="Mao Zetan">Mao Zetan</a> (毛泽覃, 1905–1935), younger brother, executed by the KMT</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mao_Zejian" title="Mao Zejian">Mao Zejian</a> (毛泽建, 1905–1929), adopted sister, executed by the KMT</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>Mao Zedong's parents altogether had five sons and two daughters. Two of the sons and both daughters died young, leaving the three brothers Mao Zedong, Mao Zemin, and Mao Zetan. Like all three of Mao Zedong's wives, Mao Zemin and Mao Zetan were communists. Like Yang Kaihui, both Zemin and Zetan were killed in warfare during Mao Zedong's lifetime.</dd>
</dl>
<p>Note that the character <i>zé</i> (泽) appears in all of the siblings' given names. This is a common Chinese naming convention.</p>
<p>From the next generation, Zemin's son, <a href="/wiki/Mao_Yuanxin" title="Mao Yuanxin">Mao Yuanxin</a>, was raised by Mao Zedong's family. He became Mao Zedong's liaison with the Politburo in 1975. In Li Zhisui's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Private_Life_of_Chairman_Mao" title="The Private Life of Chairman Mao">The Private Life of Chairman Mao</a></i>, Mao Yuanxin played a role in the final power-struggles.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267"><span>[</span>267<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Children">Children</span></h3>
<p>Mao Zedong had a total of ten children,<sup id="cite_ref-Spence_268-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spence-268"><span>[</span>268<span>]</span></a></sup> including:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mao_Anying" title="Mao Anying">Mao Anying</a> (毛岸英, 1922–1950): son to Yang, married to Liú Sīqí (刘思齐), who was born Liú Sōnglín (刘松林), <a href="/wiki/Killed_in_action" title="Killed in action">killed in action</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mao_Anqing" title="Mao Anqing">Mao Anqing</a> (毛岸青, 1923–2007): son to Yang, married to <a href="/wiki/Shao_Hua" title="Shao Hua">Shao Hua</a> (邵华), grandson <a href="/wiki/Mao_Xinyu" title="Mao Xinyu">Mao Xinyu</a> (毛新宇), great-grandson Mao Dongdong</li>
<li>Mao Anlong (1927–1931): son to Yang, died during the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Civil_War" title="Chinese Civil War">Chinese Civil War</a></li>
<li>Mao Anhong (1932–1935?): son to He, left to Mao's younger brother <a href="/wiki/Mao_Zetan" title="Mao Zetan">Zetan</a> and then to one of Zetan's guards when he went off to war, was never heard of again</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Li_Min_(politician)" title="Li Min (politician)">Li Min</a> (李敏, b. 1936): daughter to He, married to Kǒng Lìnghuá (孔令华), son Kǒng Jìníng (孔继宁), daughter Kǒng Dōngméi (孔冬梅)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Li_Na_(Daughter_of_Mao_Zedong)" title="Li Na (Daughter of Mao Zedong)" class="mw-redirect">Li Na</a> (李讷, Pinyin: Lĭ Nà, b. 1940): daughter to Jiang (whose birth given name was Li, a name also used by Mao while evading the KMT), married to Wáng Jǐngqīng (王景清), son Wáng Xiàozhī (王效芝)</li>
</ul>
<p>Mao's first and second daughters were left to local villagers because it was too dangerous to raise them while fighting the <a href="/wiki/Kuomintang" title="Kuomintang">Kuomintang</a> and later the Japanese. Their youngest daughter (born in early 1938 in Moscow after Mao separated) and one other child (born 1933) died in infancy. Two English researchers who retraced the entire Long March route in 2002–2003<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269"><span>[</span>269<span>]</span></a></sup> located a woman whom they believe might well be one of the missing children abandoned by Mao to peasants in 1935. Ed Jocelyn and Andrew McEwen hope a member of the Mao family will respond to requests for a DNA test.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270"><span>[</span>270<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Through his ten children, Mao became grandfather to twelve grandchildren, many of whom he never knew. He has many great-grandchildren alive today. One of his granddaughters is businesswoman Kong Dongmei, one of the richest people in China and mother to three of Mao's great-grandchildren.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271"><span>[</span>271<span>]</span></a></sup> His grandson <a href="/wiki/Mao_Xinyu" title="Mao Xinyu">Mao Xinyu</a> (Kong's half-brother), father of two, is a general in the Chinese army,<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272"><span>[</span>272<span>]</span></a></sup> and is often ridiculed for his weight within China.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273"><span>[</span>273<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span></h2>
<p>Mao's private life was very secretive at the time of his rule. However, after Mao's death, <a href="/wiki/Li_Zhisui" title="Li Zhisui">Li Zhisui</a>, his personal physician, published <i><a href="/wiki/The_Private_Life_of_Chairman_Mao" title="The Private Life of Chairman Mao">The Private Life of Chairman Mao</a></i>, a memoir which mentions some aspects of Mao's private life, such as chain-smoking cigarettes, rare bathing or dental habits, laziness, addiction to sleeping pills and large number of sexual partners.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274"><span>[</span>274<span>]</span></a></sup> Some scholars and some other people who also personally knew and worked with Mao, however, have disputed the accuracy of these characterizaions.<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275"><span>[</span>275<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Having grown up in <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">Hunan</a>, Mao spoke <a href="/wiki/Standard_Mandarin" title="Standard Mandarin" class="mw-redirect">Mandarin</a> with a marked Hunanese accent.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollingworth1985_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollingworth1985-276"><span>[</span>276<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ross_Terrill" title="Ross Terrill">Ross Terrill</a> noted Mao was a "son of the soil&#160;... rural and unsophisticated" in origins,<sup id="cite_ref-Terrill_19_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Terrill_19-277"><span>[</span>277<span>]</span></a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Clare_Hollingworth" title="Clare Hollingworth">Clare Hollingworth</a> asserted he was proud of his "peasant ways and manners", having a strong Hunanese accent and providing "earthy" comments on sexual matters.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollingworth1985_276-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollingworth1985-276"><span>[</span>276<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lee_Feigon" title="Lee Feigon">Lee Feigon</a> noted that Mao's "earthiness" meant that he remained connected to "everyday Chinese life."<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278"><span>[</span>278<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Biographer Peter Carter described Mao as having "an attractive personality" who could for much of the time be a "moderate and balanced man", but noted that he could also be ruthless, and showed no mercy to his opponents.<sup id="cite_ref-Carter1976_p76_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carter1976_p76-120"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> This description was echoed by Sinologist <a href="/wiki/Stuart_Schram" title="Stuart Schram" class="mw-redirect">Stuart Schram</a>, who emphasised Mao's ruthlessness, but who also noted that he showed no sign of taking pleasure in torture or killing in the revolutionary cause.<sup id="cite_ref-Schram1966_p153_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schram1966_p153-117"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> Lee Feigon considered Mao "draconian and authoritarian" when threatened, but opined that he was not the "kind of villain that his mentor Stalin was".<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279"><span>[</span>279<span>]</span></a></sup> Alexander Pantsov and Steven I. Levine claimed that Mao was a "man of complex moods", who "tried his best to bring about prosperity and gain international respect" for China, being "neither a saint nor a demon."<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280"><span>[</span>280<span>]</span></a></sup> They noted that in early life, he strived to be "a strong, wilful, and purposeful hero, not bound by any moral chains", and that he "passionately desired fame and power".<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281"><span>[</span>281<span>]</span></a></sup> Carter noted that throughout his life, Mao had the ability to gain people's trust, and that as such he gathered around him "an extraordinarily wide range of friends" in his early years.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282"><span>[</span>282<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writings_and_calligraphy">Writings and calligraphy</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Baidi_Mao.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Baidi_Mao.jpg/170px-Baidi_Mao.jpg" width="170" height="106" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Baidi_Mao.jpg/255px-Baidi_Mao.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Baidi_Mao.jpg/340px-Baidi_Mao.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="498" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Baidi_Mao.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mao's <a href="/wiki/Calligraphy" title="Calligraphy">calligraphy</a>: a bronze plaque of a poem by <a href="/wiki/Li_Bai" title="Li Bai">Li Bai</a>. (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">白帝城毛泽东手书李白诗铜匾</span></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Mao was a prolific writer of political and philosophical literature.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283"><span>[</span>283<span>]</span></a></sup> He is the attributed author of <i>Quotations from Chairman Mao Tse-tung</i>, known in the West as the "Little Red Book" and in Cultural Revolution China as the "Red Treasure Book" (红宝书): this is a collection of short extracts from his speeches and articles, edited by <a href="/wiki/Lin_Biao" title="Lin Biao">Lin Biao</a> and ordered topically. Mao wrote several other philosophical treatises, both before and after he assumed power. These include:</p>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/On_Guerrilla_Warfare" title="On Guerrilla Warfare">On Guerrilla Warfare</a></i> (《游击战》); 1937</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/On_Practice" title="On Practice">On Practice</a></i> (《实践论》); 1937</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/On_Contradiction" title="On Contradiction">On Contradiction</a></i> (《矛盾论》); 1937</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/On_Protracted_War" title="On Protracted War">On Protracted War</a></i> (《论持久战》); 1938</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Norman_Bethune#Legacy" title="Norman Bethune">In Memory of Norman Bethune</a></i> (《纪念白求恩》); 1939</li>
<li><i>On <a href="/wiki/New_Democracy" title="New Democracy">New Democracy</a></i> (《新民主主义论》); 1940</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Talks_at_the_Yan%27an_Forum_on_Literature_and_Art" title="Talks at the Yan'an Forum on Literature and Art" class="mw-redirect">Talks at the Yan'an Forum on Literature and Art</a></i> (《在延安文艺座谈会上的讲话》); 1942</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Serve_the_People" title="Serve the People">Serve the People</a></i> (《为人民服务》); 1944</li>
<li><i>The Foolish Old Man Who Removed the Mountains</i> (《愚公移山》); 1945</li>
<li><i>On the Correct Handling of the Contradictions Among the People</i> (《正确处理人民内部矛盾问题》); 1957</li>
</ul>
<p>Mao was also a skilled <a href="/wiki/Chinese_calligrapher" title="Chinese calligrapher" class="mw-redirect">Chinese calligrapher</a> with a highly personal style. In China, Mao was considered a master calligrapher during his lifetime.<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284"><span>[</span>284<span>]</span></a></sup> His calligraphy can be seen today throughout mainland China.<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285"><span>[</span>285<span>]</span></a></sup> His work gave rise to a new form of Chinese calligraphy called "Mao-style" or <i>Maoti</i>, which has gained increasing popularity since his death. There currently exist various competitions specialising in Mao-style calligraphy.<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286"><span>[</span>286<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_works">Literary works</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Poetry_of_Mao_Zedong" title="Poetry of Mao Zedong">Poetry of Mao Zedong</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Qinyuanchun_Changsha.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Qinyuanchun_Changsha.JPG/170px-Qinyuanchun_Changsha.JPG" width="170" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Qinyuanchun_Changsha.JPG/255px-Qinyuanchun_Changsha.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Qinyuanchun_Changsha.JPG/340px-Qinyuanchun_Changsha.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Qinyuanchun_Changsha.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mao's calligraphy of his poem "Qingyuanchun Changsha"</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As did most Chinese intellectuals of his generation, Mao's education began with <a href="/wiki/Chinese_classic_texts" title="Chinese classic texts" class="mw-redirect">Chinese classical literature</a>. Mao told Edgar Snow in 1936 that he had started the study of the Confucian <a href="/wiki/Analects" title="Analects">Analects</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Four_Books_and_Five_Classics" title="Four Books and Five Classics">Four Books</a> at a village school when he was eight, but that the books he most enjoyed reading were <i><a href="/wiki/Water_Margin" title="Water Margin">Water Margin</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Journey_to_the_West" title="Journey to the West">Journey to the West</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Romance_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Romance of the Three Kingdoms">Romance of the Three Kingdoms</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Dream_of_the_Red_Chamber" title="Dream of the Red Chamber">Dream of the Red Chamber</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Barnstone_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnstone-287"><span>[</span>287<span>]</span></a></sup> Mao published poems in classical forms starting in his youth and his abilities as a poet contributed to his image in China after he came to power in 1949. His style was influenced by the great <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> poets <a href="/wiki/Li_Bai" title="Li Bai">Li Bai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Li_He" title="Li He">Li He</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288"><span>[</span>288<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Some of his most well-known poems are <i><a href="/wiki/Changsha_(poem)" title="Changsha (poem)">Changsha</a></i> (1925), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Double_Ninth" title="The Double Ninth">The Double Ninth</a></i> (1929.10), <i>Loushan Pass</i> (1935), <i>The Long March</i> (1935), <i><a href="/wiki/Poetry_of_Mao_Zedong#Snow_.281936.02.29" title="Poetry of Mao Zedong">Snow</a></i> (1936), <i><a href="/wiki/Poetry_of_Mao_Zedong#The_PLA_Captures_Nanjing_.281949.04" title="Poetry of Mao Zedong">The PLA Captures Nanjing</a></i> (1949), <i>Reply to Li Shuyi</i> (1957.05.11) and <i>Ode to the Plum Blossom</i> (1961.12).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Portrayal_in_film_and_television">Portrayal in film and television</span></h2>
<p>Mao has been portrayed in film and television numerous times. Some notable actors include: Han Shi, the first actor ever to have portrayed Mao, in a 1978 drama <i>Dielianhua</i> and later again in a 1980 film <i>Cross the Dadu River</i>;<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289"><span>[</span>289<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gu_Yue" title="Gu Yue">Gu Yue</a>, who had portrayed Mao 84 times on screen throughout his 27-year career and had won the Best Actor title at the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Flowers_Awards" title="Hundred Flowers Awards">Hundred Flowers Awards</a> in 1990 and 1993;<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290"><span>[</span>290<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291"><span>[</span>291<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Liu_Ye_(actor)" title="Liu Ye (actor)">Liu Ye</a>, who played a young Mao in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Founding_of_a_Party" title="The Founding of a Party">The Founding of a Party</a></i> (2011);<sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292"><span>[</span>292<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tang_Guoqiang" title="Tang Guoqiang">Tang Guoqiang</a>, who has frequently portrayed Mao in more recent times, in the films <i>The Long March</i> (1996) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Founding_of_a_Republic" title="The Founding of a Republic">The Founding of a Republic</a></i> (2009), and the television series <i><a href="/wiki/Huang_Yanpei_(TV_series)" title="Huang Yanpei (TV series)">Huang Yanpei</a></i> (2010), among others.<sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293"><span>[</span>293<span>]</span></a></sup> Mao is a principal character in American composer <a href="/wiki/John_Adams_(composer)" title="John Adams (composer)">John Adams</a>' opera <i><a href="/wiki/Nixon_in_China" title="Nixon in China">Nixon in China</a></i> (1987).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mao_and_Tibet">Mao and Tibet</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sinicization_of_Tibet" title="Sinicization of Tibet">Sinicization of Tibet</a></div>
<p>After Mao Zedong won the Chinese civil war in 1949, his goal became the unification of the "five nationalities" under the big family, the People's Republic of China, and under a single political system, the Communist Party of China.<sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294"><span>[</span>294<span>]</span></a></sup> Aware of Mao's vision, the Tibetan government in <a href="/wiki/Lhasa" title="Lhasa">Lhasa</a> sent a representative, <a href="/wiki/Ngapoi_Ngawang_Jigme" title="Ngapoi Ngawang Jigme">Ngabo</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Chamdo" title="Chamdo">Chamdo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kham" title="Kham">Kham</a>, a strategically high valued town near the border. Ngabo had orders to hold the position while reinforcements were coming from the Lhasa and fight off the Chinese.<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295"><span>[</span>295<span>]</span></a></sup> On October 16, 1950, news came that the People's Liberation Army was advancing towards Chamdo and had also taken another strategic town named, Riwoche, which could block the route to Lhasa.<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296"><span>[</span>296<span>]</span></a></sup> With new orders, Ngabo and his men retreated to a monastery where the People's Liberation Army finally surrounded and captured them,<sup id="cite_ref-Schaik_2011_212_297-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schaik_2011_212-297"><span>[</span>297<span>]</span></a></sup> though they were treated with respect.<sup id="cite_ref-Schaik_2011_212_297-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schaik_2011_212-297"><span>[</span>297<span>]</span></a></sup> Ngabo wrote to Lhasa suggesting a peaceful surrender instead of war.<sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298"><span>[</span>298<span>]</span></a></sup> During the negotiation, the Chinese negotiator laid the cards straight on the table, "It is up to you to choose whether Tibet would be liberated peacefully or by force. It is only a matter of sending a telegram to the PLA group to recommence their march to Lhasa."<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299"><span>[</span>299<span>]</span></a></sup> Ngabo accepted Mao's "<a href="/wiki/Seventeen_Point_Agreement_for_the_Peaceful_Liberation_of_Tibet" title="Seventeen Point Agreement for the Peaceful Liberation of Tibet">Seventeen-Point Agreement</a>", which constituted Tibet as part of the People's Republic China, in return for which Tibet would be granted <a href="/wiki/Autonomy" title="Autonomy">autonomy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300"><span>[</span>300<span>]</span></a></sup> In the face of discouraging lack of support from the rest of the world, the <a href="/wiki/14th_Dalai_Lama" title="14th Dalai Lama">Dalai Lama</a> on August 1951, sent a telegram to Mao accepting the Seventeen-Point Agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301"><span>[</span>301<span>]</span></a></sup> However the delegates signing the agreement were forced to do so and the Tibetan's Government's seal used was forged.<sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302"><span>[</span>302<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

Mao got this number from a report submitted by Xu Zirong, Deputy Public Security Minister, which stated 712,000 counter-revolutionaries were executed, 1,290,000 were imprisoned, and another 1,200,000 were "subjected to control.": see <a href="#CITEREFKuisong2008">Kuisong 2008</a>.</span></li>
